      </div>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Phonetic_features">
        Phonetic features
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Phonetic features">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div style="width:52px">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" width="40"/>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">
          This section
          <b>
           contains an enumeration of examples, but lacks a general overview of its topic.
          </b>
          You can help by adding an appropriate
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style_(lists)#Lead_paragraphs_in_embedded_lists" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style (lists)">
           introductory section
          </a>
          .
          <a href="/wiki/Help:Editing" title="Help:Editing">
           Editing help
          </a>
          is available.
          <small>
           <i>
            (April 2017)
           </i>
          </small>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pardon_our_dust.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1944" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Pardon_our_dust.jpg/200px-Pardon_our_dust.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Pardon_our_dust.jpg/300px-Pardon_our_dust.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Pardon_our_dust.jpg/400px-Pardon_our_dust.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pardon_our_dust.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         First settled by the Spanish in the 16th century, today, 19% of Floridians speak Spanish, and is the most widely taught second language. In
         <a href="/wiki/Miami" title="Miami">
          Miami
         </a>
         , 67% of residents spoke Spanish as their first language in 2000.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <ul>
       <li>
        As most ancestors of Hispanic Americans came from Hispanic America, ⟨z⟩ and ⟨c⟩ (before
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Close-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close-mid front unrounded vowel">
          e
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Close_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close front unrounded vowel">
          i
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        )
        <a href="/wiki/Phonological_history_of_Spanish_coronal_fricatives" title="Phonological history of Spanish coronal fricatives">
         are pronounced
        </a>
        as
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_fricative#Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiceless alveolar fricative">
          s
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        , the same as ⟨s⟩. However,
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seseo" title="Seseo">
          seseo
         </a>
        </i>
        (replacement of
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_dental_fricative" title="Voiceless dental fricative">
          θ
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        with
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_fricative#Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiceless alveolar fricative">
          s
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        ) is also typical of the speech of Hispanic Americans of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andalusian_people" title="Andalusian people">
         Andalusian
        </a>
        and
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canarian_people" title="Canarian people">
         Canarian descent
        </a>
        . Andalusia's and the Canary Island's predominant position in the conquest and subsequent immigration to Hispanic America from Spain is thought to be the reason for the absence of this distinction in most Hispanic American dialects. Most
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_American" title="Spanish American">
         Americans of Spanish descent
        </a>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distinci%C3%B3n" title="Distinción">
         pronounce
        </a>
        the two letters as
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_fricative#Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiceless alveolar fricative">
          s
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Most speakers in Spain, particularly the regions that have a distinctive
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_dental_fricative" title="Voiceless dental fricative">
          θ
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        phoneme, realize
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_fricative#Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiceless alveolar fricative">
          s
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        with the tip of tongue against the alveolar ridge. Phonetically this is an
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_retracted_sibilant" title="Voiceless alveolar retracted sibilant">
         "apico-alveolar" "grave" sibilant
        </a>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless_apico-alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiceless apico-alveolar sibilant">
          s̺
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        , with a weak "hushing" sound reminiscent of
        <a href="/wiki/Retroflex_consonant" title="Retroflex consonant">
         retroflex
        </a>
        fricatives. To a Hispanic and Latino American speaker, European Spanish
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_fricative#Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiceless alveolar fricative">
          s
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        is close to
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_postalveolar_fricative" title="Voiceless postalveolar fricative">
          ʃ
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        like English
        <i>
         sh
        </i>
        as in
        <i>
         she
        </i>
        . However, this apico-alveolar realization of
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_fricative#Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiceless alveolar fricative">
          s
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        is not uncommon in some Latin American Spanish dialects which lack
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_dental_fricative" title="Voiceless dental fricative">
          θ
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        ; some inland Colombian Spanish (particularly Antioquia) and Andean regions of Peru and Bolivia also have an apico-alveolar
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_fricative#Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiceless alveolar fricative">
          s
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Spanish in the United States usually features
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Ye%C3%ADsmo" title="Yeísmo">
          yeísmo
         </a>
        </i>
        : there is no distinction between ⟨ll⟩ and ⟨y⟩, and both are
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Voiced_palatal_fricative#Palatal" title="Voiced palatal fricative">
          ʝ
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        . However, yeísmo is an expanding and now dominant feature of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Spanish" title="European Spanish">
         European Spanish
        </a>
        , particularly in urban speech (Madrid, Toledo) and especially in
        <a href="/wiki/Andalusia" title="Andalusia">
         Andalusia
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">
         Canary Islands
        </a>
        , though in rural use
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Palatal_lateral_approximant" title="Palatal lateral approximant">
          ʎ
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        is preserved not only in central and northern Spain but also in scatter areas of Andalusia and the Canary Islands. Speakers of
        <a href="/wiki/Rioplatense_Spanish" title="Rioplatense Spanish">
         Rioplatense Spanish
        </a>
        pronounce both ⟨ll⟩ and ⟨y⟩ as
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Voiced_postalveolar_fricative" title="Voiced postalveolar fricative">
          ʒ
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        or
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_postalveolar_fricative" title="Voiceless postalveolar fricative">
          ʃ
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        . The traditional pronunciation of the digraph ⟨ll⟩ as
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Palatal_lateral_approximant" title="Palatal lateral approximant">
          ʎ
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        is preserved in some dialects along the
        <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">
         Andes
        </a>
        range, especially in inland
        <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">
         Peru
        </a>
        and the
        <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">
         Colombia
        </a>
        highlands (Santander), northern Argentina, all
        <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">
         Bolivia
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">
         Paraguay
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Most speakers with ancestors born in coastal regions may
        <a href="/wiki/Debuccalization" title="Debuccalization">
         debuccalize
        </a>
        or
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aspiration_(linguistics)" title="Aspiration (linguistics)">
         aspirate
        </a>
        syllable-final
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_fricative#Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiceless alveolar fricative">
          s
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        to
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_glottal_fricative" title="Voiceless glottal fricative">
          h
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        , or drop it entirely, so that
        <i>
         está
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Spanish" title="Help:IPA/Spanish">
          [esˈta]
         </a>
        </span>
        ("s/he is") sounds like
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ehˈta]
        </span>
        or
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [eˈta]
        </span>
        , as in southern Spain (
        <a href="/wiki/Andalusia" title="Andalusia">
         Andalusia
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Region_of_Murcia" title="Region of Murcia">
         Murcia
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Castile%E2%80%93La_Mancha" title="Castile–La Mancha">
         Castile–La Mancha
        </a>
        (except North-East),
        <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">
         Canary Islands
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Ceuta" title="Ceuta">
         Ceuta
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Melilla" title="Melilla">
         Melilla
        </a>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        ⟨g⟩ (before
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Close-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close-mid front unrounded vowel">
          e
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        or
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Close_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close front unrounded vowel">
          i
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        ) and ⟨j⟩ are usually
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aspiration_(linguistics)" title="Aspiration (linguistics)">
         aspirated
        </a>
        to
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_glottal_fricative" title="Voiceless glottal fricative">
          h
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        in Caribbean and other coastal dialects, as well as in all Colombia, and southern Mexico, as in most southern Spanish dialects. While it may be
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_fricative" title="Voiceless velar fricative">
          x
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        in other dialects of Hispanic Americans and often
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_uvular_fricative" title="Voiceless uvular fricative">
          χ
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        in Peruvian Spanish dialect, this is a common feature of
        <a href="/wiki/Castilian_Spanish" title="Castilian Spanish">
         Castilian Spanish
        </a>
        . It is usually
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aspiration_(linguistics)" title="Aspiration (linguistics)">
         aspirated
        </a>
        to
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_glottal_fricative" title="Voiceless glottal fricative">
          h
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        as in most southwestern Spanish varieties. Very often, especially in Argentina and Chile,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_fricative" title="Voiceless velar fricative">
          x
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        becomes more fronted
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_palatal_fricative#Palatal" title="Voiceless palatal fricative">
          ç
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        when preceding high vowels
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /e, i/
        </span>
        (these speakers approach
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_fricative" title="Voiceless velar fricative">
          x
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        to the realization of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ich-Laut" title="Ich-Laut">
         German ⟨ch⟩ in
         <i>
          ich
         </i>
        </a>
        ); in other phonological environments it is pronounced either
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_fricative" title="Voiceless velar fricative">
          x
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        or
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_glottal_fricative" title="Voiceless glottal fricative">
          h
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        In many Caribbean dialects, the phonemes
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_lateral_approximant" title="Alveolar lateral approximant">
          l
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_trill" title="Alveolar trill">
          r
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        at the end of a syllable sound alike or can be exchanged:
        <i>
         caldo
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         ca[r]do
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         cardo
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         ca[l]do
        </i>
        ;
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_trill" title="Alveolar trill">
          r
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        in word-final position becomes silent, giving Caribbean dialects of Spanish a partial
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhotic_and_non-rhotic_accents" title="Rhotic and non-rhotic accents">
         non-rhoticity
        </a>
        . This happens at a reduced level in Ecuador and Chile
        <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
         [
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
           <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">
            citation needed
           </span>
          </a>
         </i>
         ]
        </sup>
        as well and is a feature brought from Extremadura and westernmost Andalusia.
       </li>
       <li>
        In many Andean regions, the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_trill" title="Alveolar trill">
         alveolar trill
        </a>
        of
        <i>
         rata
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         carro
        </i>
        is realized as an
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_approximant" title="Alveolar approximant">
         alveolar approximant
        </a>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_approximant" title="Alveolar approximant">
          ɹ
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        or even as a voiced apico-alveolar
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_fricative#Voiced_alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiced alveolar fricative">
          z
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        . The alveolar approximant realization is particularly associated with an indigenous substrate and it is quite common in Andean regions, especially in inland Ecuador, Peru, most of Bolivia and in parts of northern Argentina and Paraguay.
       </li>
       <li>
        In Puerto Rico, aside from
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_flap" title="Alveolar flap">
          ɾ
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_trill" title="Alveolar trill">
          r
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        , and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_lateral_approximant" title="Alveolar lateral approximant">
          l
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        , syllable-final
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_trill" title="Alveolar trill">
          r
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        can be realized as
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_approximant" title="Alveolar approximant">
          ɹ
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        , an influence of
        <a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">
         American English
        </a>
        on the Puerto Rican dialect;
        <i>
         "verso"'
        </i>
        (verse) becomes
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ˈbeɹso]
        </span>
        , aside from
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ˈbeɾso]
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ˈberso]
        </span>
        , or
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ˈbelso]
        </span>
        , "
        <i>
         invierno
        </i>
        " (winter) becomes
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [imˈbjeɹno]
        </span>
        , aside from
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [imˈbjeɾno]
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [imˈbjerno]
        </span>
        , or
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [imˈbjelno]
        </span>
        , and "
        <i>
         escarlata
        </i>
        " (scarlet) becomes
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ehkaɹˈlata]
        </span>
        , aside from
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ehkaɾˈlata]
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ehkarˈlata]
        </span>
        , or [
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         ehkaˈlata
        </span>
        ]. In word-final position,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_trill" title="Alveolar trill">
          r
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        will usually be one of these:
        <ul>
         <li>
          a trill, a tap, approximant,
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           [
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_lateral_approximant" title="Alveolar lateral approximant">
            l
           </a>
           ]
          </span>
          , or elided when followed by a consonant or a pause, as in
          <i>
           amo
          </i>
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           [r ~ ɾ ~ ɹ ~ l]
          </span>
          <i>
           paterno
          </i>
          'paternal love',
          <i>
           amor
          </i>
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           [aˈmo]
          </span>
          ,
         </li>
         <li>
          a tap, approximant, or
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           [
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_lateral_approximant" title="Alveolar lateral approximant">
            l
           </a>
           ]
          </span>
          when the followed by a vowel-initial word, as in
          <i>
           amo
          </i>
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           [ɾ ~ ɹ ~ l]
          </span>
          <i>
           eterno
          </i>
          'eternal love').
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Voice_(phonetics)" title="Voice (phonetics)">
         voiced
        </a>
        consonants
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Voiced_bilabial_stop" title="Voiced bilabial stop">
          b
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Voiced_dental_and_alveolar_stops#Alveolar" title="Voiced dental and alveolar stops">
          d
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        , and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_stop" title="Voiced velar stop">
          ɡ
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        are pronounced as
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plosive" title="Plosive">
         plosives
        </a>
        after and sometimes before any consonant in most
        <a href="/wiki/Colombian_Spanish" title="Colombian Spanish">
         Colombian Spanish
        </a>
        dialects (rather than the
        <a href="/wiki/Fricative_consonant" title="Fricative consonant">
         fricative
        </a>
        or
        <a href="/wiki/Approximant_consonant" title="Approximant consonant">
         approximant
        </a>
        that is characteristic of most other dialects):
        <i>
         pardo
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ˈpaɾ
         <b>
          d
         </b>
         o]
        </span>
        ,
        <i>
         barba
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ˈbaɾ
         <b>
          b
         </b>
         a]
        </span>
        ,
        <i>
         algo
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ˈal
         <b>
          ɡ
         </b>
         o]
        </span>
        ,
        <i>
         peligro
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [peˈli
         <b>
          ɡ
         </b>
         ɾo]
        </span>
        ,
        <i>
         desde
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ˈdez
         <b>
          d
         </b>
         e/ˈdeh
         <b>
          d
         </b>
         e]
        </span>
        —rather than the
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ˈpaɾ
         <b>
          ð
         </b>
         o]
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ˈbaɾ
         <b>
          β
         </b>
         a]
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ˈal
         <b>
          ɣ
         </b>
         o]
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [peˈli
         <b>
          ɣ
         </b>
         ɾo]
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ˈdez
         <b>
          ð
         </b>
         e/ˈdeh
         <b>
          ð
         </b>
         e]
        </span>
        of Spain and the rest of Spanish America. A notable exception is the
        <a href="/wiki/Nari%C3%B1o_Department" title="Nariño Department">
         Department of Nariño
        </a>
        and most
        <a href="/wiki/Colombian_Spanish#Caribbean_dialect" title="Colombian Spanish">
         <i>
          Costeño
         </i>
         speech (Atlantic coastal dialects)
        </a>
        which feature the soft, fricative realizations common to all other Hispanic American and European dialects.
       </li>
       <li>
        Word-final
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Dental,_alveolar_and_postalveolar_nasals#Alveolar" title="Dental, alveolar and postalveolar nasals">
          n
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        is frequently velar
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Velar_nasal" title="Velar nasal">
          ŋ
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        in Latin American Spanish; this means a word like
        <i>
         pan
        </i>
        (bread) is often articulated
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         ['paŋ]
        </span>
        . To an English-speaker, those speakers that have a velar nasal for word-final
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Dental,_alveolar_and_postalveolar_nasals#Alveolar" title="Dental, alveolar and postalveolar nasals">
          n
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        make
        <i>
         pan
        </i>
        sound like
        <i>
         pang
        </i>
        . Velarization of word-final
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Dental,_alveolar_and_postalveolar_nasals#Alveolar" title="Dental, alveolar and postalveolar nasals">
          n
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        is so widespread in the Americas that it is easier to mention those regions that maintain an alveolar, European-style,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Dental,_alveolar_and_postalveolar_nasals#Alveolar" title="Dental, alveolar and postalveolar nasals">
          n
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        : most of Mexico, Colombia (except for coastal dialects) and Argentina (except for some northern regions). Elsewhere, velarization is common, though alveolar word-final
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Dental,_alveolar_and_postalveolar_nasals#Alveolar" title="Dental, alveolar and postalveolar nasals">
          n
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        can appear among some educated speakers, especially in the media or in singing. Velar word-final
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Dental,_alveolar_and_postalveolar_nasals#Alveolar" title="Dental, alveolar and postalveolar nasals">
          n
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        is also frequent in Spain, especially in southern Spanish dialects (Andalusia and the Canary Islands) and also in the Northwest: Galicia, Asturias and León.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Lexical_features">
        Lexical features
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Lexical features">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The usage of Spanish words in American bilinguals shows a convergence of
       <a href="/wiki/Semantics" title="Semantics">
        semantics
       </a>
       between English and Spanish
       <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">
        cognates
       </a>
       . For example, the Spanish words
       <i>
        atender
       </i>
       ("to pay attention to") and
       <i>
        éxito
       </i>
       ("success") acquire a similar semantic range in American Spanish to the English words "attend" and "exit". In some cases,
       <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">
        loanwords
       </a>
       from English give existing Spanish words a
       <a href="/wiki/Homonym" title="Homonym">
        homonymic
       </a>
       meaning: so while
       <i>
        coche
       </i>
       has come to acquire the additional meaning of "coach" in the United States, it retains its older meaning of "car".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Disappearance of
        <i>
         de
        </i>
        (of) in certain expressions, as is the case with the
        <a href="/wiki/Canarian_Spanish" title="Canarian Spanish">
         dialect of Spanish in the Canary Islands
        </a>
        . Example:
        <i>
         esposo Rosa
        </i>
        instead of
        <i>
         esposo de Rosa
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         gofio millo
        </i>
        instead of
        <i>
         gofio de millo
        </i>
        , etc.
       </li>
       <li>
        Doublets of Arabic-Latinate synonyms with the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">
         Arabic
        </a>
        form are more common in American Spanish, which derives from Latin American Spanish and so influenced by Andalusian Spanish like Andalusian and Latin American
        <i>
         alcoba
        </i>
        for standard
        <i>
         habitación
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         dormitorio
        </i>
        ('bedroom') or
        <i>
         alhaja
        </i>
        for standard
        <i>
         joya
        </i>
        ('jewel').
       </li>
       <li>
        See
        <a href="/wiki/Spanish_dialects_and_varieties#Vocabulary" title="Spanish dialects and varieties">
         List of words having different meanings in Spain and Hispanic America
        </a>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Future_of_Spanish_in_the_United_States">
        Future of Spanish in the United States
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Future of Spanish in the United States">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Spanish-speaking Americans are the fastest growing linguistic group in the United States. Continual immigration and prevalent Spanish-language mass media (such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Univisi%C3%B3n" title="Univisión">
        Univisión
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Telemundo" title="Telemundo">
        Telemundo
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Azteca_Am%C3%A9rica" title="Azteca América">
        Azteca América
       </a>
       ) support the Spanish-speaking populations. Moreover, because of the
       <a href="/wiki/North_American_Free_Trade_Agreement" title="North American Free Trade Agreement">
        North American Free Trade Agreement
       </a>
       , it is common for many American manufacturers to use multilingual product labeling using English, French and Spanish, three of
       <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_American_States#Official_languages" title="Organization of American States">
        the four official languages
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_American_States" title="Organization of American States">
        Organization of American States
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Besides the businesses that always have catered to Hispanophone immigrants, a small, but increasing, number of mainstream American retailers now advertise bilingually in Spanish-speaking areas and offer bilingual, English-Spanish customer services. One common indicator of such businesses is
       <i>
        Se Habla Español
       </i>
       which means "Spanish Is Spoken".
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spanishuspsposter.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="722" data-file-width="600" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Spanishuspsposter.jpg/220px-Spanishuspsposter.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Spanishuspsposter.jpg/330px-Spanishuspsposter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Spanishuspsposter.jpg/440px-Spanishuspsposter.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spanishuspsposter.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Federal agencies such as the
         <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postal_Service" title="United States Postal Service">
          United States Postal Service
         </a>
         translate information into Spanish.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_the_Union_Address" title="State of the Union Address">
        State of the Union Addresses
       </a>
       and other presidential speeches are translated into Spanish, following the precedent set by the
       <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">
        Bill Clinton
       </a>
       administration. Moreover, non-Hispanic American origin politicians fluent in Spanish speak in Spanish to Hispanic majority
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constituency" title="Constituency">
        constituencies
       </a>
       . There are 500 Spanish newspapers, 152 magazines, and 205 publishers in the United States; magazine and local television advertising expenditures for the Hispanic market have increased substantially from 1999 to 2003, with growth of 58 percent and 43 percent, respectively.
      </p>
      <p>
       Historically, immigrants' languages tend to disappear or become reduced through generational
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assimilation_(linguistics)" title="Assimilation (linguistics)">
        assimilation
       </a>
       . Spanish disappeared in several countries and U.S. territories during the 20th century, notably in the
       <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">
        Philippines
       </a>
       and in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific_Island" title="Pacific Island">
        Pacific Island
       </a>
       countries of
       <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">
        Guam
       </a>
       , Micronesia,
       <a href="/wiki/Palau" title="Palau">
        Palau
       </a>
       , the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Marianas" title="Northern Marianas">
        Northern Marianas
       </a>
       islands, and the
       <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Islands" title="Marshall Islands">
        Marshall Islands
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/English-only_movement" title="English-only movement">
        English-only movement
       </a>
       seeks to establish English as the sole official language of the United States. Generally, they exert political public pressure upon Hispanophone immigrants to learn English and speak it publicly; as universities, business, and the professions use English, there is much social pressure to learn English for upward socio-economic mobility.
      </p>
      <p>
       Generally, Hispanic American origin US residents (13.4% of the 2002 population) are bilingual to a degree. A Simmons Market Research survey recorded that 19 percent of the Hispanic American origin population speak only Spanish, 9 percent speak only English, 55 percent have limited English proficiency, and 17 percent are fully English-Spanish bilingual.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Intergenerational transmission of Spanish is a more accurate indicator of Spanish's future in the United States than raw statistical numbers of Hispanophone immigrants. Although Hispanic American origin immigrants hold varying English proficiency levels, almost all second-generation Hispanic American origin U.S. residents speak English, yet about 50 percent speak Spanish at home. Two-thirds of third-generation
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexican_American" title="Mexican American">
        Mexican Americans
       </a>
       speak only English at home.
       <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Veltman" title="Calvin Veltman">
        Calvin Veltman
       </a>
       undertook in 1988, for the National Center for Education Statistics and for the Hispanic Policy Development Project, the most complete study of English language adoption by Hispanophone immigrants. Veltman's
       <a href="/wiki/Language_shift" title="Language shift">
        language shift
       </a>
       studies document abandonment of Spanish at rates of 40 percent for immigrants who arrived in the U.S. before the age of 14, and 70 percent for immigrants who arrived before the age of 10.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The complete set of these studies' demographic projections postulates the near-complete assimilation of a given Hispanophone immigrant cohort within two generations. Although his study based itself upon a large 1976 sample from the Bureau of the Census (which has not been repeated), data from the 1990 Census tend to confirm the great
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglicization" title="Anglicization">
        Anglicization
       </a>
       of the U.S. Hispanic American origin population.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="American_literature_in_Spanish">
        American literature in Spanish
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: American literature in Spanish">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Cuban_American_literature" title="Cuban American literature">
        Cuban American literature
       </a>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Southwest_Colonial_literature">
        Southwest Colonial literature
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Southwest Colonial literature">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In 1610,
       <a href="/wiki/Gaspar_P%C3%A9rez_de_Villagr%C3%A1" title="Gaspar Pérez de Villagrá">
        Gaspar Pérez de Villagrá
       </a>
       published his
       <i>
        Historia de Nuevo México
       </i>
       (History of New Mexico).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">
        19th century
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: 19th century">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In 1880,
       <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mart%C3%AD" title="José Martí">
        José Martí
       </a>
       moved to New York City.
      </p>
      <p>
       Eusebio Chacón published
       <i>
        El hijo de la tempestad
       </i>
       in 1892.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">
        20th century
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: 20th century">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Federico_Garc%C3%ADa_Lorca" title="Federico García Lorca">
        Federico García Lorca
       </a>
       wrote his collection of poems,
       <i>
        Poeta en Nueva York
       </i>
       , and the two plays
       <i>
        Así que pasen cinco años
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        El público
       </i>
       while living in New York.
       <a href="/wiki/Giannina_Braschi" title="Giannina Braschi">
        Giannina Braschi
       </a>
       wrote the Hispanic postmodern poetry classic
       <i>
        El imperio de los sueños
       </i>
       in Spanish in New York.
       <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Vasconcelos" title="José Vasconcelos">
        José Vasconcelos
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Juan_Ram%C3%B3n_Jim%C3%A9nez" title="Juan Ramón Jiménez">
        Juan Ramón Jiménez
       </a>
       were both
       <a href="/wiki/Exile" title="Exile">
        exiled
       </a>
       to the United States.
      </p>
      <p>
       In her autobiography
       <i>
        When I was Puerto Rican
       </i>
       (1993), Esmeralda Santiago recounts her childhood on the island during the 1950s and her family's subsequent move to New York City, when she was 13 years old. Originally written in English, the book is an example of New York Rican literature.
      </p>
